<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Italian sculptor, painter, architect and poet (1475‚Äì1564)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Michelangelo_(disambiguation)" title="Michelangelo (disambiguation)">Michelangelo (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Michelangelo di Lodovico Buonarroti Simoni</b> (<small>Italian:¬†</small><span class="IPA" lang="it-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[mikeÀàland íelo di lodoÀàviÀêko Àåbw…înarÀàr…îÀêti siÀàmoÀêni]</a></span>; 6 March 1475¬†‚Äì 18 February 1564), known simply as <b>Michelangelo</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small>English: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àå/: secondary stress follows">Àå</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/a…™/: 'i' in 'tide'">a…™</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/…ôl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">…ôl</span><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="/√¶/: 'a' in 'bad'">√¶</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/d í/: 'j' in 'jam'">d í</span><span title="/…ô/: 'a' in 'about'">…ô</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/o ä/: 'o' in 'code'">o ä</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àå/: secondary stress follows">Àå</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/…™/: 'i' in 'kit'">…™</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>-/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>), was an Italian sculptor, painter, architect and poet of the <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a>. Born in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a>, his work had a major influence on the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art" title="Western art">Western art</a>, particularly in relation to the Renaissance notions of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">naturalism</a>. He is often considered a contender for the title of the archetypal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_man" title="Renaissance man">Renaissance man</a>, along with his rival and elder contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> Given the sheer volume of surviving correspondence, sketches, and reminiscences, Michelangelo is one of the best-documented artists of the 16th century and several scholars have described Michelangelo as the most accomplished artist of his era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>He sculpted two of his best-known works, the <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0_(Michelangelo)" title="Piet√† (Michelangelo)">Piet√†</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a></i>, before the age of thirty. Despite holding a low opinion of painting, he also created two of the most influential <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescoes</a> in the history of Western art: the scenes from Genesis on the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">ceiling of the Sistine Chapel</a> in Rome, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Judgment_(Michelangelo)" title="The Last Judgment (Michelangelo)">The Last Judgment</a></i> on its altar wall. His design of the <a href="/wiki/Laurentian_Library" title="Laurentian Library">Laurentian Library</a> pioneered <a href="/wiki/Mannerism#Mannerist_architecture" title="Mannerism">Mannerist architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> At the age of 71, he succeeded <a href="/wiki/Antonio_da_Sangallo_the_Younger" title="Antonio da Sangallo the Younger">Antonio da Sangallo the Younger</a> as the architect of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>. He transformed the plan so that the western end was finished to his design, as was the dome, with some modification, after his death.
</p><p>Michelangelo was the first Western artist whose <a href="/wiki/Biography" title="Biography">biography</a> was published while he was alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> In fact, two biographies were published during his lifetime. One of them, by <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a>, proposed that Michelangelo's work transcended that of any artist living or dead, and was "supreme in not one art alone but in all three."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his lifetime, Michelangelo was often called <i>Il Divino</i> ("the divine one").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> His contemporaries often admired his <i>terribilit√†</i>‚Äîhis ability to instill a sense of awe in viewers of his art. Attempts by subsequent artists to imitate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Michelangelo's impassioned, highly personal style contributed to the rise of <a href="/wiki/Mannerism" title="Mannerism">Mannerism</a>, a short-lived style and period in Western art following the High Renaissance.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Early_life.2C_1475.E2.80.931488"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life,_1475‚Äì1488">Early life, 1475‚Äì1488</span></h3>
<p>Michelangelo was born on 6 March 1475<sup class="reference" id="ref_anone"><a href="#endnote_anone">[a]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Caprese_Michelangelo" title="Caprese Michelangelo">Caprese</a>, known today as Caprese Michelangelo, a small town situated in Valtiberina,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> near <a href="/wiki/Arezzo" title="Arezzo">Arezzo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay11_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay11-10">[10]</a></sup> For several generations, his family had been small-scale bankers in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>; but the bank failed, and his father, Ludovico di Leonardo Buonarroti Simoni, briefly took a government post in Caprese, where Michelangelo was born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> At the time of Michelangelo's birth, his father was the town's <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">judicial administrator</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Podest%C3%A0" title="Podest√†">podest√†</a></i> or local administrator of <a href="/wiki/Chiusi_della_Verna" title="Chiusi della Verna">Chiusi della Verna</a>. Michelangelo's mother was Francesca di Neri del Miniato di Siena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clement5_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-clement5-11">[11]</a></sup> The Buonarrotis claimed to descend from the Countess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathilde_of_Canossa" title="Mathilde of Canossa">Mathilde of Canossa</a>‚Äîa claim that remains unproven, but which Michelangelo believed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several months after Michelangelo's birth, the family returned to Florence, where he was raised. During his mother's later prolonged illness, and after her death in 1481 (when he was six years old), Michelangelo lived with a nanny and her husband, a stonecutter, in the town of <a href="/wiki/Settignano" title="Settignano">Settignano</a>, where his father owned a marble quarry and a small farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clement5_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-clement5-11">[11]</a></sup> There he gained his love for marble. As <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> quotes him:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If there is some good in me, it is because I was born in the subtle atmosphere of your country of Arezzo. Along with the milk of my nurse I received the knack of handling chisel and hammer, with which I make my figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay11_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay11-10">[10]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="Apprenticeships.2C_1488.E2.80.931492"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Apprenticeships,_1488‚Äì1492">Apprenticeships, 1488‚Äì1492</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buonarotti-scala.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="375" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Buonarotti-scala.jpg/170px-Buonarotti-scala.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Buonarotti-scala.jpg/255px-Buonarotti-scala.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Buonarotti-scala.jpg/340px-Buonarotti-scala.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buonarotti-scala.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_of_the_Stairs" title="Madonna of the Stairs">Madonna of the Stairs</a></i> (1490‚Äì1492), Michelangelo's earliest known work in marble</div></div></div>
<p>As a young boy, Michelangelo was sent to <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> to study <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Humanism" title="Renaissance Humanism">Humanist</a> Francesco da Urbino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay11_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay11-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi9_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi9-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_bnone"><a href="#endnote_bnone">[b]</a></sup> However, he showed no interest in his schooling, preferring to copy paintings from churches and seek the company of other painters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi9_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi9-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city of Florence was at that time Italy's greatest centre of the arts and learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan14_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan14-14">[14]</a></sup> Art was sponsored by the Signoria (the town council), the merchant guilds, and wealthy patrons such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medici" title="Medici">Medici</a> and their banking associates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan35_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan35-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, a renewal of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical</a> scholarship and the arts, had its first flowering in Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan14_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan14-14">[14]</a></sup> In the early 15th century, the architect <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a>, having studied the remains of Classical buildings in Rome, had created two churches, <a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo,_Florence" title="San Lorenzo, Florence">San Lorenzo's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santo_Spirito,_Florence" title="Santo Spirito, Florence">Santo Spirito</a>, which embodied the Classical precepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The sculptor <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Lorenzo Ghiberti">Lorenzo Ghiberti</a> had laboured for fifty years to create the north and east bronze doors of the <a href="/wiki/Florence_Baptistery" title="Florence Baptistery">Baptistry</a>, which Michelangelo was to describe as "The Gates of Paradise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The exterior niches of the Church of <a href="/wiki/Orsanmichele" title="Orsanmichele">Orsanmichele</a> contained a gallery of works by the most acclaimed sculptors of Florence: <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>, Ghiberti, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Verrocchio" title="Andrea del Verrocchio">Andrea del Verrocchio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nanni_di_Banco" title="Nanni di Banco">Nanni di Banco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan35_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan35-15">[15]</a></sup> The interiors of the older churches were covered with frescos (mostly in Late Medieval, but also in the Early Renaissance style), begun by <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> and continued by <a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Brancacci_Chapel" title="Brancacci Chapel">Brancacci Chapel</a>, both of whose works Michelangelo studied and copied in drawings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan28_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan28-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>During Michelangelo's childhood, a team of painters had been called from Florence to the Vatican to decorate the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel" title="Sistine Chapel">Sistine Chapel</a>. Among them was <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Ghirlandaio" title="Domenico Ghirlandaio">Domenico Ghirlandaio</a>, a master in fresco painting, perspective, figure drawing and portraiture who had the largest workshop in Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan35_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan35-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1488, at age 13, Michelangelo was apprenticed to Ghirlandaio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liebert59_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liebert59-19">[19]</a></sup> The next year, his father persuaded Ghirlandaio to pay Michelangelo as an artist, which was rare for someone of fourteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> When in 1489, <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo de' Medici">Lorenzo de' Medici</a>, de facto ruler of Florence, asked Ghirlandaio for his two best pupils, Ghirlandaio sent Michelangelo and <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Granacci" title="Francesco Granacci">Francesco Granacci</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1490 to 1492, Michelangelo attended the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy_(Florence)" title="Platonic Academy (Florence)">Platonic Academy</a>, a Humanist academy founded by the Medici. There, his work and outlook were influenced by many of the most prominent philosophers and writers of the day, including <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Marsilio Ficino</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pico_della_Mirandola" title="Pico della Mirandola">Pico della Mirandola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poliziano" title="Poliziano">Poliziano</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay1819_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay1819-22">[22]</a></sup> At this time, Michelangelo sculpted the reliefs <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madonna_of_the_Steps" title="Madonna of the Steps">Madonna of the Steps</a></i> (1490‚Äì1492) and <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Centaurs_(Michelangelo)" title="Battle of the Centaurs (Michelangelo)">Battle of the Centaurs</a></i> (1491‚Äì1492),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlan28_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coughlan28-18">[18]</a></sup> the latter based on a theme suggested by Poliziano and commissioned by Lorenzo de' Medici.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi15_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi15-23">[23]</a></sup> Michelangelo worked for a time with the sculptor <a href="/wiki/Bertoldo_di_Giovanni" title="Bertoldo di Giovanni">Bertoldo di Giovanni</a>. When he was seventeen, another pupil, <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Torrigiano" title="Pietro Torrigiano">Pietro Torrigiano</a>, struck him on the nose, causing the disfigurement that is conspicuous in the portraits of Michelangelo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bologna.2C_Florence_and_Rome.2C_1492.E2.80.931499"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bologna,_Florence_and_Rome,_1492‚Äì1499">Bologna, Florence and Rome, 1492‚Äì1499</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1660" data-file-width="1584" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/280px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/420px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg/560px-Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_Pieta_5450_cropncleaned_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0_(Michelangelo)" title="Piet√† (Michelangelo)">Piet√†</a></i>, St Peter's Basilica (1498‚Äì99)</div></div></div>
<p>Lorenzo de' Medici's death on 8 April 1492 brought a reversal of Michelangelo's circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay2021_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay2021-25">[25]</a></sup> Michelangelo left the security of the Medici court and returned to his father's house. In the following months he carved a polychrome wooden <i><a href="/wiki/Crucifix_(Michelangelo)" title="Crucifix (Michelangelo)">Crucifix</a></i> (1493), as a gift to the prior of the Florentine church of Santo Spirito, which had allowed him to do some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical" title="Anatomical">anatomical</a> studies of the corpses from the church's hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi17_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi17-26">[26]</a></sup> This was the first of several instances during his career that Michelangelo studied anatomy by dissecting cadavers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1493 and 1494, he bought a block of marble, and carved a larger-than-life statue of <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Hercules</a>, which was sent to France and subsequently disappeared sometime in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi15_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi15-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_cnone"><a href="#endnote_cnone">[c]</a></sup> On 20 January 1494, after heavy snowfalls, Lorenzo's heir, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piero_di_Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Piero di Lorenzo de' Medici">Piero de Medici</a>, commissioned a statue made of snow, and Michelangelo again entered the court of the Medici.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the same year, the Medici were expelled from Florence as the result of the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savonarola" title="Savonarola">Savonarola</a>. Michelangelo left the city before the end of the political upheaval, moving to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay2021_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay2021-25">[25]</a></sup> In Bologna, he was commissioned to carve several of the last small figures for the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Arca_di_San_Domenico" title="Arca di San Domenico">Shrine of St. Dominic</a>, in the church dedicated to that saint. At this time Michelangelo studied the robust reliefs carved by <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_della_Quercia" title="Jacopo della Quercia">Jacopo della Quercia</a> around the main portal of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Petronio_Basilica" title="San Petronio Basilica">Basilica of St Petronius</a>, including the panel of <i>The Creation of Eve</i>, the composition of which was to reappear on the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Towards the end of 1495, the political situation in Florence was calmer; the city, previously under threat from the French, was no longer in danger as <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII</a> had suffered defeats. Michelangelo returned to Florence but received no commissions from the new city government under Savonarola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> He returned to the employment of the Medici.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay2425_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay2425-32">[32]</a></sup> During the half-year he spent in Florence, he worked on two small statues, a child <i>St. John the Baptist</i> and a sleeping <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cupid_(Michelangelo)" title="Cupid (Michelangelo)">Cupid</a></i>. According to Condivi, <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_di_Pierfrancesco_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo di Pierfrancesco de' Medici">Lorenzo di Pierfrancesco de' Medici</a>, for whom Michelangelo had sculpted <i>St. John the Baptist</i>, asked that Michelangelo "fix it so that it looked as if it had been buried" so he could "send it to Rome¬†... pass [it off as] an ancient work and¬†... sell it much better." Both Lorenzo and Michelangelo were unwittingly cheated out of the real value of the piece by a middleman. Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Raffaele_Riario" title="Raffaele Riario">Raffaele Riario</a>, to whom Lorenzo had sold it, discovered that it was a fraud, but was so impressed by the quality of the sculpture that he invited the artist to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi1920_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi1920-33">[33]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="ref_dnone"><a href="#endnote_dnone">[d]</a></sup> This apparent success in selling his sculpture abroad as well as the conservative Florentine situation may have encouraged Michelangelo to accept the prelate's invitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay2425_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay2425-32">[32]</a></sup>
Michelangelo arrived in Rome on 25 June 1496<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tolnay2628_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tolnay2628-34">[34]</a></sup> at the age of 21. On 4 July of the same year, he began work on a commission for the banker Jacopo Galli, for his garden, an over-life-size statue of the Roman wine god <i><a href="/wiki/Bacchus_(Michelangelo)" title="Bacchus (Michelangelo)">Bacchus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1497, the French ambassador to the Holy See, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bilh%C3%A8res_de_Lagraulas" title="Jean Bilh√®res de Lagraulas">Jean de Bilh√®res-Lagraulas</a>, commissioned him to carve a <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0_(Michelangelo)" title="Piet√† (Michelangelo)">Piet√†</a></i>, a sculpture showing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Mary" title="St Mary">Virgin Mary</a> grieving over the body of Jesus. The subject, which is not part of the Biblical narrative of the Crucifixion, was common in religious sculpture of Medieval Northern Europe and would have been very familiar to the Cardinal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirst47_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirst47-36">[36]</a></sup> The contract was agreed upon in August of the following year. Michelangelo was 24 at the time of its completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirst47_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hirst47-36">[36]</a></sup> It was soon to be regarded as one of the world's great masterpieces of sculpture, "a revelation of all the potentialities and force of the art of sculpture". Contemporary opinion was summarised by Vasari: "It is certainly a miracle that a formless block of stone could ever have been reduced to a perfection that nature is scarcely able to create in the flesh."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It is now located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St Peter's Basilica">St Peter's Basilica</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Florence.2C_1499.E2.80.931505"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Florence,_1499‚Äì1505">Florence, 1499‚Äì1505</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David (Michelangelo)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg/220px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg/330px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg/440px-%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27David%27_by_Michelangelo_Fir_JBU005_denoised.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">Statue of David</a></i>, completed by Michelangelo in 1504, is one of the most renowned works of the Renaissance.</div></div></div>
<p>Michelangelo returned to Florence in 1499. The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic</a> was changing after the fall of its leader, anti-Renaissance priest <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Savonarola" title="Girolamo Savonarola">Girolamo Savonarola</a>, who was executed in 1498, and the rise of the <i>gonfaloniere</i> <a href="/wiki/Piero_Soderini" title="Piero Soderini">Piero Soderini</a>. Michelangelo was asked by the consuls of the Guild of Wool to complete an unfinished project begun 40 years earlier by <a href="/wiki/Agostino_di_Duccio" title="Agostino di Duccio">Agostino di Duccio</a>: a colossal statue of <a href="/wiki/Carrara_marble" title="Carrara marble">Carrara marble</a> portraying David as a symbol of Florentine freedom to be placed on the gable of <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paoletti387_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paoletti387-38">[38]</a></sup> Michelangelo responded by completing his most famous work, the <a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">statue of David</a>, in 1504. The masterwork definitively established his prominence as a sculptor of extraordinary technical skill and strength of symbolic imagination. A team of consultants, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botticelli" title="Botticelli">Botticelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filippino_Lippi" title="Filippino Lippi">Filippino Lippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Perugino" title="Pietro Perugino">Pietro Perugino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_di_Credi" title="Lorenzo di Credi">Lorenzo di Credi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_da_Sangallo_the_Elder" title="Antonio da Sangallo the Elder">Antonio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_da_Sangallo" title="Giuliano da Sangallo">Giuliano da Sangallo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_della_Robbia" title="Andrea della Robbia">Andrea della Robbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_Rosselli" title="Cosimo Rosselli">Cosimo Rosselli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Davide_Ghirlandaio" title="Davide Ghirlandaio">Davide Ghirlandaio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piero_di_Cosimo" title="Piero di Cosimo">Piero di Cosimo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Sansovino" title="Andrea Sansovino">Andrea Sansovino</a> and Michelangelo's dear friend <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Granacci" title="Francesco Granacci">Francesco Granacci</a>, was called together to decide upon its placement, ultimately the Piazza della Signoria, in front of the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Vecchio" title="Palazzo Vecchio">Palazzo Vecchio</a>. It now stands in the <a href="/wiki/Galleria_dell%27Accademia" title="Galleria dell'Accademia">Academia</a> while a replica occupies its place in the square.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
In the same period of placing the <a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">David</a>, Michelangelo may have been involved in creating the <a href="/wiki/Importuno_di_Michelangelo" title="Importuno di Michelangelo">sculptural profile on Palazzo Vecchio</a>'s fa√ßade known as the <a href="/wiki/Importuno_di_Michelangelo" title="Importuno di Michelangelo">Importuno di Michelangelo</a>. The hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[40]</a></sup> on Michelangelo's possible involvement in the creation of the profile is based on the strong resemblance of the latter to a profile drawn by the artist, datable to the beginning of the 16th century, now preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the completion of the <i>David</i> came another commission. In early 1504 Leonardo da Vinci had been commissioned to paint <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Anghiari_(painting)" title="The Battle of Anghiari (painting)">The Battle of Anghiari</a></i> in the council chamber of the Palazzo Vecchio, depicting the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Anghiari" title="Battle of Anghiari">battle between Florence and Milan</a> in 1440. Michelangelo was then commissioned to paint the <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cascina_(Michelangelo)" title="Battle of Cascina (Michelangelo)">Battle of Cascina</a></i>. The two paintings are very different: Leonardo depicts soldiers fighting on horseback, while Michelangelo has soldiers being ambushed as they bathe in the river. Neither work was completed and both were lost forever when the chamber was refurbished. Both works were much admired, and copies remain of them, Leonardo's work having been copied by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubens" title="Rubens">Rubens</a> and Michelangelo's by <a href="/wiki/Bastiano_da_Sangallo" title="Bastiano da Sangallo">Bastiano da Sangallo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also during this period, Michelangelo was commissioned by Angelo Doni to paint a "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Family" title="Holy Family">Holy Family</a>" as a present for his wife, Maddalena Strozzi. It is known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Doni_Tondo" title="Doni Tondo">Doni Tondo</a></i> and hangs in the Uffizi Gallery in its original magnificent frame, which Michelangelo may have designed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider,_p._11_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider,_p._11-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> He also may have painted the Madonna and Child with <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Madonna" title="Manchester Madonna">Manchester Madonna</a></i> and now in the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery" title="National Gallery">National Gallery</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Tomb_of_Julius_II.2C_1505.E2.80.931545"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomb_of_Julius_II,_1505‚Äì1545">Tomb of Julius II, 1505‚Äì1545</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Pope_Julius_II" title="Tomb of Pope Julius II">Tomb of Pope Julius II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3776" data-file-width="2520" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg/220px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg/330px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg/440px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Julius_II" title="Tomb of Julius II">Tomb of Julius II</a>, 1505‚Äì1545</div></div></div>
<p>In 1505 Michelangelo was invited back to Rome by the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a> and commissioned to build the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Pope_Julius_II" title="Tomb of Pope Julius II">Pope's tomb</a>, which was to include forty statues and be finished in five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldcheider14_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldcheider14-46">[46]</a></sup> Under the patronage of the pope, Michelangelo experienced constant interruptions to his work on the tomb in order to accomplish numerous other tasks.
</p><p>The commission for the tomb forced the artist to leave <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> with his planned <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cascina_(Michelangelo)" title="Battle of Cascina (Michelangelo)"><i>Battle of Cascina</i></a> painting unfinished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-49">[49]</a></sup> By this time, Michelangelo was established as an artist;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> both he and Julius II had hot tempers and soon argued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-49">[49]</a></sup> On 17 April 1506, Michelangelo left Rome in secret for Florence, remaining there until the Florentine government pressed him to return to the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Michelangelo worked on the tomb for 40 years, it was never finished to his satisfaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldcheider14_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldcheider14-46">[46]</a></sup> It is located in the <a href="/wiki/San_Pietro_in_Vincoli" title="San Pietro in Vincoli">Church of San Pietro in Vincoli</a> in Rome and is most famous for the central <a href="/wiki/Moses_(Michelangelo)" title="Moses (Michelangelo)">figure of Moses</a>, completed in 1516.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> Of the other statues intended for the tomb, two, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Rebellious_Slave" title="Rebellious Slave">Rebellious Slave</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Slave" title="Dying Slave">Dying Slave</a></i>, are now in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldcheider14_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldcheider14-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sistine_Chapel_ceiling.2C_1505.E2.80.931512"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sistine_Chapel_ceiling,_1505‚Äì1512">Sistine Chapel ceiling, 1505‚Äì1512</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg/330px-Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg/495px-Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg/660px-Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vatican-ChapelleSixtine-Plafond.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Michelangelo painted the ceiling of the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel" title="Sistine Chapel">Sistine Chapel</a>; the work took approximately four years to complete (1508‚Äì1512)</div></div></div>
<p>During the same period, Michelangelo painted the ceiling of the Sistine Chapel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0b_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0b-52">[52]</a></sup> which took approximately four years to complete (1508‚Äì1512).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> According to Condivi's account, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bramante" title="Bramante">Bramante</a>, who was working on the building of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, resented Michelangelo's commission for the pope's tomb and convinced the pope to commission him in a medium with which he was unfamiliar, in order that he might fail at the task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Michelangelo was originally commissioned to paint the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" title="Twelve Apostles">Twelve Apostles</a> on the triangular <a href="/wiki/Pendentive" title="Pendentive">pendentives</a> that supported the ceiling, and to cover the central part of the ceiling with ornament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider12_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider12-54">[54]</a></sup> Michelangelo persuaded Pope Julius II to give him a free hand and proposed a different and more complex scheme,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-49">[49]</a></sup> representing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_story" title="Genesis creation story">Creation</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_Man" title="Fall of Man">Fall of Man</a>, the Promise of Salvation through the prophets, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Christ" title="Genealogy of Christ">genealogy of Christ</a>. The work is part of a larger scheme of decoration within the chapel that represents much of the doctrine of the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider12_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider12-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The composition stretches over 500 square metres of ceiling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and contains over 300 figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider12_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider12-54">[54]</a></sup> At its centre are nine episodes from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a>, divided into three groups: God's creation of the earth; God's creation of humankind and their fall from God's grace; and lastly, the state of humanity as represented by <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a> and his family. On the pendentives supporting the ceiling are painted twelve men and women who prophesied the coming of Jesus, seven <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> of Israel, and five <a href="/wiki/Sibyl" title="Sibyl">Sibyls</a>, prophetic women of the Classical world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider12_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider12-54">[54]</a></sup> Among the most famous paintings on the ceiling are <a href="/wiki/The_Creation_of_Adam" title="The Creation of Adam">The Creation of Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deluge_myth" title="Deluge myth">Deluge</a>, the Prophet <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cumaean_Sibyl" title="Cumaean Sibyl">Cumaean Sibyl</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Florence_under_Medici_popes.2C_1513_.E2.80.93_early_1534"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Florence_under_Medici_popes,_1513_‚Äì_early_1534">Florence under Medici popes, 1513 ‚Äì early 1534</span></h3>
<p>In 1513, Pope Julius II died and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a>, the second son of Lorenzo de' Medici.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> From 1513 to 1516, Pope Leo was on good terms with Pope Julius's surviving relatives, so encouraged Michelangelo to continue work on Julius's tomb, but the families became enemies again in 1516 when Pope Leo tried to seize the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Urbino" title="Duchy of Urbino">Duchy of Urbino</a> from Julius's nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francesco_Maria_I_della_Rovere" title="Francesco Maria I della Rovere">Francesco Maria I della Rovere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Pope Leo then had Michelangelo stop working on the tomb, and commissioned him to reconstruct the fa√ßade of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Lorenzo,_Florence" title="Basilica of San Lorenzo, Florence">Basilica of San Lorenzo in Florence</a> and to adorn it with sculptures. He spent three years creating drawings and models for the fa√ßade, as well as attempting to open a new marble quarry at <a href="/wiki/Pietrasanta" title="Pietrasanta">Pietrasanta</a> specifically for the project. In 1520, the work was abruptly cancelled by his financially strapped patrons before any real progress had been made. The basilica lacks a fa√ßade to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1520, the Medici came back to Michelangelo with another grand proposal, this time for a family funerary chapel in the Basilica of San Lorenzo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> For posterity, this project, occupying the artist for much of the 1520s and 1530s, was more fully realised. Michelangelo used his own discretion to create the composition of the <a href="/wiki/Medici_Chapel" title="Medici Chapel">Medici Chapel</a>, which houses the large tombs of two of the younger members of the Medici family, <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_de%27_Medici,_Duke_of_Nemours" title="Giuliano de' Medici, Duke of Nemours">Giuliano, Duke of Nemours</a>, and Lorenzo, his nephew. It also serves to commemorate their more famous predecessors, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorenzo_the_Magnificent" title="Lorenzo the Magnificent">Lorenzo the Magnificent</a> and his brother Giuliano, who are buried nearby. The tombs display statues of the two Medici and allegorical figures representing <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Night_(Michelangelo)" title="Night (Michelangelo)">Night</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_(Michelangelo)" title="Day (Michelangelo)">Day</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dusk_(Michelangelo)" title="Dusk (Michelangelo)">Dusk</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dawn_(Michelangelo)" title="Dawn (Michelangelo)">Dawn</a></i>. The chapel also contains Michelangelo's <i>Medici Madonna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In 1976, a concealed corridor was discovered with drawings on the walls that related to the chapel itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pope Leo X died in 1521 and was succeeded briefly by the austere <a href="/wiki/Pope_Adrian_VI" title="Pope Adrian VI">Adrian VI</a>, and then by his cousin Giulio Medici as <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1524, Michelangelo received an architectural commission from the Medici pope for the <a href="/wiki/Laurentian_Library" title="Laurentian Library">Laurentian Library</a> at San Lorenzo's Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> He designed both the interior of the library itself and its vestibule, a building utilising architectural forms with such dynamic effect that it is seen as the forerunner of <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a>. It was left to assistants to interpret his plans and carry out construction. The library was not opened until 1571, and the vestibule remained incomplete until 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1527, Florentine citizens, encouraged by the <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(1527)" title="Sack of Rome (1527)">sack of Rome</a>, threw out the Medici and restored the republic. A siege of the city ensued, and Michelangelo went to the aid of his beloved Florence by working on the city's fortifications from 1528 to 1529. The city fell in 1530, and the Medici were restored to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> Michelangelo fell out of favour with the young Alessandro Medici, who had been installed as the first Duke of Florence. Fearing for his life, he fled to Rome, leaving assistants to complete the Medici chapel and the Laurentian Library. Despite Michelangelo's support of the republic and resistance to the Medici rule, he was welcomed by Pope Clement, who reinstated an allowance that he had previously granted the artist and made a new contract with him over the tomb of Pope Julius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Rome.2C_1534.E2.80.931546"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rome,_1534‚Äì1546">Rome, 1534‚Äì1546</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4395" data-file-width="3950" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg/280px-Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg/420px-Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg/560px-Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Last_Judgement_by_Michelangelo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Judgment_(Michelangelo)" title="The Last Judgment (Michelangelo)">The Last Judgment</a></i> (1534‚Äì1541)</div></div></div>
<p>In Rome, Michelangelo lived near the church of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_di_Loreto_(Rome)" title="Santa Maria di Loreto (Rome)">Santa Maria di Loreto</a>. It was at this time that he met the poet <a href="/wiki/Vittoria_Colonna" title="Vittoria Colonna">Vittoria Colonna</a>, marchioness of <a href="/wiki/Pescara" title="Pescara">Pescara</a>, who was to become one of his closest friends until her death in 1547.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A._Condivi_p._103_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-A._Condivi_p._103-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly before his death in 1534, Pope Clement VII commissioned Michelangelo to paint a fresco of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Judgment_(Michelangelo)" title="The Last Judgment (Michelangelo)">The Last Judgment</a></i> on the altar wall of the Sistine Chapel. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>, was instrumental in seeing that Michelangelo began and completed the project, which he laboured on from 1534 to October 1541.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> The fresco depicts the Second Coming of Christ and his Judgement of the souls. Michelangelo ignored the usual artistic conventions in portraying Jesus, showing him as a massive, muscular figure, youthful, beardless and naked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz100_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz100-65">[65]</a></sup> He is surrounded by saints, among whom <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Bartholomew" title="Saint Bartholomew">Saint Bartholomew</a> holds a drooping flayed skin, bearing the likeness of Michelangelo. The dead rise from their graves, to be consigned either to Heaven or to Hell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz100_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz100-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once completed, the depiction of Christ and the Virgin Mary naked was considered sacrilegious, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_IV" title="Pope Paul IV">Cardinal Carafa</a> and Monsignor Sernini (<a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">Mantua</a>'s ambassador) campaigned to have the fresco removed or censored, but the Pope resisted. At the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>, shortly before Michelangelo's death in 1564, it was decided to obscure the genitals and <a href="/wiki/Daniele_da_Volterra" title="Daniele da Volterra">Daniele da Volterra</a>, an apprentice of Michelangelo, was commissioned to make the alterations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> An uncensored copy of the original, by <a href="/wiki/Marcello_Venusti" title="Marcello Venusti">Marcello Venusti</a>, is in the <a href="/wiki/Museo_di_Capodimonte" title="Museo di Capodimonte">Capodimonte Museum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG19_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-LG19-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Michelangelo worked on a number of architectural projects at this time. They included a design for the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitoline Hill</a> with its trapezoid piazza displaying the ancient bronze statue of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>. He designed the upper floor of the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Farnese" title="Palazzo Farnese">Palazzo Farnese</a> and the interior of the Church of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_degli_Angeli_e_dei_Martiri" title="Santa Maria degli Angeli e dei Martiri">Santa Maria degli Angeli</a>, in which he transformed the vaulted interior of an Ancient Roman bathhouse. Other architectural works include <a href="/wiki/San_Giovanni_dei_Fiorentini" title="San Giovanni dei Fiorentini">San Giovanni dei Fiorentini</a>, the Sforza Chapel (Capella Sforza) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore" title="Basilica di Santa Maria Maggiore">Basilica di Santa Maria Maggiore</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Porta_Pia" title="Porta Pia">Porta Pia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="St_Peter.27s_Basilica.2C_1546.E2.80.931564"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="St_Peter's_Basilica,_1546‚Äì1564">St Peter's Basilica, 1546‚Äì1564</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Basilica#Architecture" title="St Peter's Basilica">St Peter's Basilica ¬ß¬†Architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3557" data-file-width="2668" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg/220px-Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg/330px-Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg/440px-Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basilique_Saint-Pierre_Vatican_dome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dome of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St Peter's Basilica">St Peter's Basilica</a></div></div></div>
<p>While still working on the <i>Last Judgment</i>, Michelangelo received yet another commission for the Vatican. This was for the painting of two large frescos in the Cappella Paolina depicting significant events in the lives of the two most important saints of Rome, the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conversion_of_Saul_(Michelangelo)" title="The Conversion of Saul (Michelangelo)">Conversion of Saint Paul</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Crucifixion_of_St._Peter_(Michelangelo)" title="The Crucifixion of St. Peter (Michelangelo)">Crucifixion of Saint Peter</a></i>. Like the <i>Last Judgment</i>, these two works are complex compositions containing a great number of figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They were completed in 1550. In the same year, Giorgio Vasari published his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Eminent_Painters,_Sculptors,_and_Architects" title="Lives of the Most Eminent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects">Vita</a></i>, including a biography of Michelangelo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1546, Michelangelo was appointed architect of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>, Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-7"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> The process of replacing the Constantinian basilica of the 4th century had been underway for fifty years and in 1506 foundations had been laid to the plans of Bramante. Successive architects had worked on it, but little progress had been made. Michelangelo was persuaded to take over the project. He returned to the concepts of Bramante, and developed his ideas for a centrally planned church, strengthening the structure both physically and visually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The dome, not completed until after his death, has been called by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banister_Fletcher" title="Banister Fletcher">Banister Fletcher</a>, "the greatest creation of the Renaissance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>As construction was progressing on St Peter's, there was concern that Michelangelo would pass away before the dome was finished. However, once building commenced on the lower part of the dome, the supporting ring, the completion of the design was inevitable.
</p><p>On 7 December 2007, a red chalk sketch for the dome of St Peter's Basilica, possibly the last made by Michelangelo before his death, was discovered in the Vatican archives. It is extremely rare, since he destroyed his designs later in life. The sketch is a partial plan for one of the radial columns of the cupola drum of St Peter's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_ignudo_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1176" data-file-width="748" decoding="async" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg/220px-Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg/330px-Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg/440px-Michelangelo%2C_ignudo_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_ignudo_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling#Ignudi" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Ignudo</a></i> fresco from 1509 on the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_(%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1168" data-file-width="1208" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png/220px-Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png/330px-Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png/440px-Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_%28%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michael_Angelus_Pictor_-_Francisco_de_Holanda_(%C3%81lbum_dos_Desenhos_das_Antigualhas).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Michelangelo, drawn from sight by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Holanda" title="Francisco de Holanda">Francisco de Holanda</a> in the late 1530s.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg/220px-Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg/330px-Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg/440px-Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_de%E2%80%98_Cavalieri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing showing <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_dei_Cavalieri" title="Tommaso dei Cavalieri">Tommaso dei Cavalieri</a> by Michelangelo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1209" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg/220px-Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg/330px-Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg/440px-Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo_Buonarroti_-_Tityus_-_WGA15503.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tityus_(Michelangelo)" title="Tityus (Michelangelo)">The Punishment of Tityus</a>, gift to Tommaso dei Cavalieri, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ1532</span></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith">Faith</span></h3>
<p>Michelangelo was a devout Catholic whose faith deepened at the end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> His poetry includes the following closing lines from what is known as poem 285 (written in 1554): "Neither painting nor sculpture will be able any longer to calm my soul, now turned toward that divine love that opened his arms on the cross to take us in."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_habits">Personal habits</span></h3>
<p>Michelangelo was abstemious in his personal life, and once told his apprentice, <a href="/wiki/Ascanio_Condivi" title="Ascanio Condivi">Ascanio Condivi</a>: "However rich I may have been, I have always lived like a poor man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CondiviLOM106_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CondiviLOM106-79">[79]</a></sup> Michelangelo's bank accounts and numerous deeds of purchase show that his net worth was about 50,000 gold <a href="/wiki/Ducat" title="Ducat">ducats</a>, more than many princes and dukes of his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Condivi said he was indifferent to food and drink, eating "more out of necessity than of pleasure"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CondiviLOM106_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-CondiviLOM106-79">[79]</a></sup> and that he "often slept in his clothes and¬†... boots."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CondiviLOM106_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-CondiviLOM106-79">[79]</a></sup> His biographer <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Giovio" title="Paolo Giovio">Paolo Giovio</a> says, "His nature was so rough and uncouth that his domestic habits were incredibly squalid, and deprived posterity of any pupils who might have followed him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This, however, may not have affected him, as he was by nature a solitary and melancholy person, <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">bizzarro e fantastico</i></span>, a man who "withdrew himself from the company of men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Condivi_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Condivi-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationships_and_poetry">Relationships and poetry</span></h3>
<p>It is impossible to know for certain whether Michelangelo had physical relationships (<a href="/wiki/Ascanio_Condivi" title="Ascanio Condivi">Condivi</a> ascribed to him a "monk-like chastity");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> speculation about his sexuality is rooted in his poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> He wrote over three hundred sonnets and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/madrigal" title="wikt:madrigal">madrigals</a>. The longest sequence, displaying deep romantic feeling, was written to the young Roman patrician <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_dei_Cavalieri" title="Tommaso dei Cavalieri">Tommaso dei Cavalieri</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ1509‚Äì1587</span>), who was 23 years old when Michelangelo first met him in 1532, at the age of 57.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The Florentine <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Varchi" title="Benedetto Varchi">Benedetto Varchi</a> fifteen years later described Cavalieri as of "incomparable beauty", with "graceful manners, so excellent an endowment and so charming a demeanour that he indeed deserved, and still deserves, the more to be loved the better he is known".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In his "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Artists" title="Lives of the Artists">Lives of the Artists</a>", <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> observed: "But infinitely more than any of the others he loved M. Tommaso de' Cavalieri, a Roman gentleman, for whom, being a young man and much inclined to these arts, [Michelangelo] made, to the end that he might learn to draw, many most superb drawings of divinely beautiful heads, designed in black and red chalk; and then he drew for him a Ganymede rapt to Heaven by Jove's Eagle, a Tityus with the Vulture devouring his heart, the Chariot of the Sun falling with Pha√´thon into the Po, and a Bacchanal of children, which are all in themselves most rare things, and drawings the like of which have never been seen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Scholars agree that Michelangelo became infatuated with Cavalieri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> 
The poems to Cavalieri make up the first large sequence of poems in any modern tongue addressed by one man to another; they predate by 50 years <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_sonnets" title="Shakespeare's sonnets">sonnets</a> to the fair youth:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>I feel as lit by fire a cold countenance<br/>
That burns me from afar and keeps itself ice-chill;<br/>
A strength I feel two shapely arms to fill<br/>
Which without motion moves every balance.
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">‚Äî‚Äâ<cite>Michael Sullivan, translation</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Cavalieri replied: "I swear to return your love. Never have I loved a man more than I love you, never have I wished for a friendship more than I wish for yours." Cavalieri remained devoted to Michelangelo until his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1542, Michelangelo met <a href="/wiki/Cecchino_dei_Bracci" title="Cecchino dei Bracci">Cecchino dei Bracci</a> who died only a year later, inspiring Michelangelo to write 48 funeral <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/epigram" title="wikt:epigram">epigrams</a>. Some of the objects of Michelangelo's affections, and subjects of his poetry, took advantage of him: the model <a href="/wiki/Febo_di_Poggio" title="Febo di Poggio">Febo di Poggio</a> asked for money in response to a love-poem, and a second model, <a href="/wiki/Gherardo_Perini" title="Gherardo Perini">Gherardo Perini</a>, shamelessly stole from him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>What some have interpreted as the seemingly <a href="/wiki/Homoeroticism" title="Homoeroticism">homoerotic</a> nature of the poetry has been a source of discomfort to later generations. Michelangelo's grandnephew, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo_Buonarroti_the_Younger" title="Michelangelo Buonarroti the Younger">Michelangelo Buonarroti the Younger</a>, published the poems in 1623 with the gender of pronouns changed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and it was not until <a href="/wiki/John_Addington_Symonds" title="John Addington Symonds">John Addington Symonds</a> translated them into English in 1893 that the original genders were restored. In modern times some scholars insist that, despite the restoration of the pronouns, they represent "an emotionless and elegant re-imagining of Platonic dialogue, whereby erotic poetry was seen as an expression of refined sensibilities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes,_Anthony_p._326-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Late in life, Michelangelo nurtured a great platonic love for the poet and noble widow <a href="/wiki/Vittoria_Colonna" title="Vittoria Colonna">Vittoria Colonna</a>, whom he met in Rome in 1536 or 1538 and who was in her late forties at the time. They wrote sonnets for each other and were in regular contact until she died. These sonnets mostly deal with the spiritual issues that occupied them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Condivi recalls Michelangelo's saying that his sole regret in life was that he did not kiss the widow's face in the same manner that he had her hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A._Condivi_p._103_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-A._Condivi_p._103-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feuds_with_other_artists">Feuds with other artists</span></h3>
<p>In a letter from late 1542, Michelangelo blamed the tensions between Julius¬†II and himself on the envy of Bramante and <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, saying of the latter, "all he had in art, he got from me". According to <a href="/wiki/Gian_Paolo_Lomazzo" title="Gian Paolo Lomazzo">Gian Paolo Lomazzo</a>, Michelangelo and Raphael met once: the former was alone, while the latter was accompanied by several others. Michelangelo commented that he thought he had encountered the chief of police with such an assemblage, and Raphael replied that he thought he had met an executioner, as they are wont to walk alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Michelangelo" title="List of works by Michelangelo">List of works by Michelangelo</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Michelangelo_Buonarroti_catalogue_raisonn%C3%A9,_2007" title="commons:Michelangelo Buonarroti catalogue raisonn√©, 2007">Michelangelo Buonarroti catalogue raisonn√©</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Madonna_and_Child">Madonna and Child</span></h3>
<p>The <i>Madonna of the Steps</i> is Michelangelo's earliest known work in marble. It is carved in shallow relief, a technique often employed by the master-sculptor of the early 15th century, Donatello, and others such as <a href="/wiki/Desiderio_da_Settignano" title="Desiderio da Settignano">Desiderio da Settignano</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> While the Madonna is in profile, the easiest aspect for a shallow relief, the child displays a twisting motion that was to become characteristic of Michelangelo's work. The <i><a href="/wiki/Taddei_Tondo" title="Taddei Tondo">Taddei Tondo</a></i> of 1502 shows the Christ Child frightened by a <a href="/wiki/Bullfinch" title="Bullfinch">Bullfinch</a>, a symbol of the Crucifixion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider,_p._11_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider,_p._11-43">[43]</a></sup> The lively form of the child was later adapted by Raphael in the <i><a href="/wiki/Bridgewater_Madonna" title="Bridgewater Madonna">Bridgewater Madonna</a></i>. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bruges_Madonna" title="Bruges Madonna">Bruges Madonna</a></i> was, at the time of its creation, unlike other such statues depicting the Virgin proudly presenting her son. Here, the Christ Child, restrained by his mother's clasping hand, is about to step off into the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Doni_Tondo" title="Doni Tondo">Doni Tondo</a></i>, depicting the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Family" title="Holy Family">Holy Family</a>, has elements of all three previous works: the frieze of figures in the background has the appearance of a low-relief, while the circular shape and dynamic forms echo the Taddeo Tondo. The twisting motion present in the <i>Bruges Madonna</i> is accentuated in the painting. The painting heralds the forms, movement and colour that Michelangelo was to employ on the ceiling of the Sistine Chapel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider,_p._11_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider,_p._11-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Male_figure">Male figure</span></h3>
<p>The kneeling angel is an early work, one of several that Michelangelo created as part of a large decorative scheme for the <a href="/wiki/Arca_di_San_Domenico" title="Arca di San Domenico">Arca di San Domenico</a> in the church dedicated to that saint in Bologna. Several other artists had worked on the scheme, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Pisano" title="Nicola Pisano">Nicola Pisano</a> in the 13th century. In the late 15th century, the project was managed by <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_dell%27Arca" title="Niccol√≤ dell'Arca">Niccol√≤ dell'Arca</a>. An angel holding a candlestick, by Niccol√≤, was already in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG9_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-LG9-96">[96]</a></sup> Although the two angels form a pair, there is a great contrast between the two works, the one depicting a delicate child with flowing hair clothed in Gothic robes with deep folds, and Michelangelo's depicting a robust and muscular youth with eagle's wings, clad in a garment of Classical style.
Everything about Michelangelo's angel is dynamic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
Michelangelo's <i><a href="/wiki/Bacchus_(Michelangelo)" title="Bacchus (Michelangelo)">Bacchus</a></i> was a commission with a specified subject, the youthful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacchus" title="Bacchus">God of Wine</a>. The sculpture has all the traditional attributes, a vine wreath, a cup of wine and a fawn, but Michelangelo ingested an air of reality into the subject, depicting him with bleary eyes, a swollen bladder and a stance that suggests he is unsteady on his feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG9_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-LG9-96">[96]</a></sup> While the work is plainly inspired by Classical sculpture, it is innovative for its rotating movement and strongly three-dimensional quality, which encourages the viewer to look at it from every angle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Slave" title="Dying Slave">Dying Slave</a></i>, Michelangelo again utilised the figure with marked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraposto" title="Contraposto">contrapposto</a> to suggest a particular human state, in this case waking from sleep. With the <i><a href="/wiki/Rebellious_Slave" title="Rebellious Slave">Rebellious Slave</a></i>, it is one of two such earlier figures for the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Pope_Julius_II" title="Tomb of Pope Julius II">Tomb of Pope Julius II</a>, now in the Louvre, that the sculptor brought to an almost finished state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> These two works were to have a profound influence on later sculpture, through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodin" title="Rodin">Rodin</a> who studied them at the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Atlas_Slave" title="Atlas Slave">Atlas Slave</a></i> is one of the later figures for Pope Julius' tomb. The works, known collectively as <i>The Captives</i>, each show the figure struggling to free itself, as if from the bonds of the rock in which it is lodged. The works give a unique insight into the sculptural methods that Michelangelo employed and his way of revealing what he perceived within the rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
