<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sistine_Chapel_ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling" title="Sistine Chapel ceiling">Sistine Chapel ceiling</a></div>
<p>The Sistine Chapel ceiling was painted between 1508 and 1512.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartz134_51-8"><a href="#cite_note-Bartz134-51">[51]</a></sup> The ceiling is a flattened <a href="/wiki/Barrel_vault" title="Barrel vault">barrel vault</a> supported on twelve triangular <a href="/wiki/Pendentive" title="Pendentive">pendentives</a> that rise from between the windows of the chapel. The commission, as envisaged by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a>, was to adorn the pendentives with figures of the twelve apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Michelangelo, who was reluctant to take the job, persuaded the Pope to give him a free hand in the composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR402_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-PR402-103">[103]</a></sup> The resultant scheme of decoration awed his contemporaries and has inspired other artists ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The scheme is of nine panels illustrating episodes from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a>, set in an architectonic frame. On the pendentives, Michelangelo replaced the proposed Apostles with Prophets and Sibyls who heralded the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR402_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-PR402-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:992px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="3129" decoding="async" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg/990px-CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg/1485px-CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg/1980px-CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg 2x" width="990"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CAPPELLA_SISTINA_Ceiling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Sistine Chapel Ceiling</i> (1508‚Äì1512)</div></div></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>Michelangelo began painting with the later episodes in the narrative, the pictures including locational details and groups of figures, the <i>Drunkenness of Noah</i> being the first of this group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR402_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-PR402-103">[103]</a></sup> In the later compositions, painted after the initial scaffolding had been removed, Michelangelo made the figures larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR402_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-PR402-103">[103]</a></sup> One of the central images, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Creation_of_Adam" title="The Creation of Adam">The Creation of Adam</a></i> is one of the best known and most reproduced works in the history of art. The final panel, showing the <i><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_Light_from_Darkness" title="Separation of Light from Darkness">Separation of Light from Darkness</a></i> is the broadest in style and was painted in a single day. As the model for the Creator, Michelangelo has depicted himself in the action of painting the ceiling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR402_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-PR402-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:52px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivresse_de_noe.jpg" title="The Drunkenness of Noah"><img alt="The Drunkenness of Noah" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="539" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ivresse_de_noe.jpg/200px-Ivresse_de_noe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ivresse_de_noe.jpg/300px-Ivresse_de_noe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ivresse_de_noe.jpg/400px-Ivresse_de_noe.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Day_of_Creation.jpg" title="The First Day of Creation"><img alt="The First Day of Creation" data-file-height="997" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/First_Day_of_Creation.jpg/164px-First_Day_of_Creation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/First_Day_of_Creation.jpg/245px-First_Day_of_Creation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/First_Day_of_Creation.jpg/327px-First_Day_of_Creation.jpg 2x" width="164"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i>The First Day of Creation</i>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>As supporters to the smaller scenes, Michelangelo painted twenty youths who have variously been interpreted as angels, as muses, or simply as decoration. Michelangelo referred to them as "ignudi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The figure reproduced may be seen in context in the above image of the <i>Separation of Light from Darkness</i>.
In the process of painting the ceiling, Michelangelo made studies for different figures, of which some, such as that for <i>The Libyan Sibyl</i> have survived, demonstrating the care taken by Michelangelo in details such as the hands and feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The Prophet <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>, contemplating the downfall of Jerusalem, is an image of the artist himself.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg" title="Ignudo"><img alt="Ignudo" data-file-height="2953" data-file-width="2214" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg/150px-%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg/225px-%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg/300px-%27Ignudo%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU33.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i>Ignudo</i>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Figure_compositions">Figure compositions</span></h3>
<p>Michelangelo's relief of the <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Centaurs_(Michelangelo)" title="Battle of the Centaurs (Michelangelo)">Battle of the Centaurs</a></i>, created while he was still a youth associated with the Medici Academy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> is an unusually complex relief in that it shows a great number of figures involved in a vigorous struggle. Such a complex disarray of figures was rare in Florentine art, where it would usually only be found in images showing either the <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_the_Innocents" title="Massacre of the Innocents">Massacre of the Innocents</a> or the Torments of Hell. The relief treatment, in which some of the figures are boldly projecting, may indicate Michelangelo's familiarity with Roman <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> reliefs from the collection of Lorenzo Medici, and similar marble panels created by <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Pisano" title="Nicola Pisano">Nicola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pisano" title="Giovanni Pisano">Giovanni Pisano</a>, and with the figurative compositions on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghiberti" title="Ghiberti">Ghiberti</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Florence_Baptistery#Lorenzo_Ghiberti" title="Florence Baptistery">Baptistry Doors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The composition of the <i>Battle of Cascina</i> is known in its entirety only from copies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> as the original cartoon, according to Vasari, was so admired that it deteriorated and was eventually in pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasari_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari-110">[110]</a></sup> It reflects the earlier relief in the energy and diversity of the figures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> with many different postures, and many being viewed from the back, as they turn towards the approaching enemy and prepare for battle.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In <i>The Last Judgment</i> it is said that Michelangelo drew <a href="/wiki/Artistic_inspiration" title="Artistic inspiration">inspiration</a> from a fresco by <a href="/wiki/Melozzo_da_Forl%C3%AC" title="Melozzo da Forl√¨">Melozzo da Forl√¨</a> in Rome's <a href="/wiki/Santi_Apostoli,_Rome" title="Santi Apostoli, Rome">Santi Apostoli</a>. Melozzo had depicted figures from different angles, as if they were floating in the Heaven and seen from below. Melozzo's majestic figure of Christ, with windblown cloak, demonstrates a degree of foreshortening of the figure that had also been employed by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Andrea Mantegna</a>, but was not usual in the frescos of Florentine painters. In <i>The Last Judgment</i> Michelangelo had the opportunity to depict, on an unprecedented scale, figures in the action of either rising heavenward or falling and being dragged down.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the two frescos of the Pauline Chapel, <i>The Crucifixion of St. Peter</i> and <i>The Conversion of Saul</i>, Michelangelo has used the various groups of figures to convey a complex narrative. In the <i>Crucifixion of Peter</i> soldiers busy themselves about their assigned duty of digging a post hole and raising the cross while various people look on and discuss the events. A group of horrified women cluster in the foreground, while another group of Christians is led by a tall man to witness the events. In the right foreground, Michelangelo walks out of the painting with an expression of disillusionment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<p>Michelangelo's architectural commissions included a number that were not realised, notably the fa√ßade for Brunelleschi's Church of San Lorenzo in Florence, for which Michelangelo had a wooden model constructed, but which remains to this day unfinished rough brick. At the same church, Giulio de' Medici (later Pope Clement VII) commissioned him to design the Medici Chapel and the tombs of Giuliano and Lorenzo Medici.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Pope Clement also commissioned the Laurentian Library, for which Michelangelo also designed the extraordinary vestibule with columns recessed into niches, and a staircase that appears to spill out of the library like a flow of lava, according to <a href="/wiki/Nikolaus_Pevsner" title="Nikolaus Pevsner">Nikolaus Pevsner</a>, "...¬†revealing <a href="/wiki/Mannerism" title="Mannerism">Mannerism</a> in its most sublime architectural form."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1546 Michelangelo produced the highly complex ovoid design for the pavement of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campidoglio" title="Campidoglio">Campidoglio</a> and began designing an upper storey for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farnese_Palace" title="Farnese Palace">Farnese Palace</a>. In 1547 he took on the job of completing St Peter's Basilica, begun to a design by Bramante, and with several intermediate designs by several architects. Michelangelo returned to Bramante's design, retaining the basic form and concepts by simplifying and strengthening the design to create a more dynamic and unified whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gardner_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gardner-114">[114]</a></sup> Although the late 16th-century engraving depicts the dome as having a hemispherical profile, the dome of Michelangelo's model is somewhat ovoid and the final product, as completed by <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_della_Porta" title="Giacomo della Porta">Giacomo della Porta</a>, is more so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gardner_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gardner-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:39.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg" title="The vestibule of the Laurentian Library has Mannerist features which challenge the Classical order of Brunelleschi's adjacent church."><img alt="The vestibule of the Laurentian Library has Mannerist features which challenge the Classical order of Brunelleschi's adjacent church." data-file-height="502" data-file-width="666" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg/200px-Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg/300px-Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg/400px-Laurentian_Library_vestibule.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span></h3>
<p>In his old age, Michelangelo created a number of <i>Piet√†s</i> in which he apparently reflects upon mortality. They are heralded by the <i>Victory</i>, perhaps created for the tomb of Pope Julius II but left unfinished. In this group, the youthful victor overcomes an older hooded figure, with the features of Michelangelo.
</p><p>The <i>Piet√† of Vittoria Colonna</i> is a chalk drawing of a type described as "presentation drawings", as they might be given as a gift by an artist, and were not necessarily studies towards a painted work. In this image, Mary's upraised arms and hands are indicative of her prophetic role. The frontal aspect is reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Masaccio" title="Masaccio">Masaccio</a>'s fresco of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity" title="Holy Trinity">Holy Trinity</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Maria_Novella" title="Basilica of Santa Maria Novella">Basilica of Santa Maria Novella</a>, Florence.
</p><p>In the <i>Florentine Piet√†</i>, Michelangelo again depicts himself, this time as the aged <a href="/wiki/Nicodemus" title="Nicodemus">Nicodemus</a> lowering the body of Jesus from the cross into the arms of Mary his mother and Mary Magdalene. Michelangelo smashed the left arm and leg of the figure of Jesus. His pupil <a href="/wiki/Tiberio_Calcagni" title="Tiberio Calcagni">Tiberio Calcagni</a> repaired the arm and drilled a hole in which to fix a replacement leg which was not subsequently attached. He also worked on the figure of Mary Magdalene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The last sculpture that Michelangelo worked on (six days before his death), the <i><a href="/wiki/Rondanini_Piet%C3%A0" title="Rondanini Piet√†">Rondanini Piet√†</a></i> could never be completed because Michelangelo carved it away until there was insufficient stone. The legs and a detached arm remain from a previous stage of the work. As it remains, the sculpture has an abstract quality, in keeping with 20th-century concepts of sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Michelangelo died in Rome in 1564, at the age of 88 (three weeks before his 89th birthday). His body was taken from Rome for interment at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce</a>, fulfilling the maestro's last request to be buried in his beloved <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Michelangelo's heir Lionardo Buonarroti commissioned <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> to design and build the <i>Tomb of Michelangelo</i>, a monumental project that cost 770 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scudi" title="Scudi">scudi</a>, and took over 14 years to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-120">[120]</a></sup> Marble for the tomb was supplied by <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_I_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo I de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo I de' Medici</a>, Duke of Tuscany who had also organized a state funeral to honour Michelangelo in Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 972px;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2802" data-file-width="1818" decoding="async" height="339" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/220px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/330px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg/440px-Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo%27s_grave4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Tomb of Michelangelo</i> (1578) by <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Michelangelo, with Leonardo da Vinci and Raphael, is one of the three giants of the Florentine <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a>. Although their names are often cited together, Michelangelo was younger than Leonardo by 23 years, and older than Raphael by eight. Because of his reclusive nature, he had little to do with either artist and outlived both of them by more than forty years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Michelangelo took few sculpture students. He employed <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Granacci" title="Francesco Granacci">Francesco Granacci</a>, who was his fellow pupil at the Medici Academy, and became one of several assistants on the Sistine Chapel ceiling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldscheider12_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-Goldscheider12-54">[54]</a></sup> Michelangelo appears to have used assistants mainly for the more manual tasks of preparing surfaces and grinding colours. Despite this, his works were to have a great influence on painters, sculptors and architects for many generations to come.
</p><p>While Michelangelo's <i>David</i> is the most famous male nude of all time and now graces cities around the world, some of his other works have had perhaps even greater impact on the course of art. The twisting forms and tensions of the <i>Victory</i>, the <i>Bruges Madonna</i> and the <i>Medici Madonna</i> make them the heralds of the <a href="/wiki/Mannerism" title="Mannerism">Mannerist art</a>. The unfinished giants for the tomb of Pope Julius II had profound effect on late-19th- and 20th-century sculptors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodin" title="Rodin">Rodin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moore" title="Henry Moore">Henry Moore</a>.
</p><p>Michelangelo's foyer of the Laurentian Library was one of the earliest buildings to utilise Classical forms in a plastic and expressive manner. This dynamic quality was later to find its major expression in Michelangelo's centrally planned St Peter's, with its <a href="/wiki/Giant_order" title="Giant order">giant order</a>, its rippling cornice and its upward-launching pointed dome. The dome of St Peter's was to influence the building of churches for many centuries, including <a href="/wiki/Sant%27Andrea_della_Valle" title="Sant'Andrea della Valle">Sant'Andrea della Valle</a> in Rome and <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a>, London, as well as the civic domes of many public buildings and the state capitals across America.
</p><p>Artists who were directly influenced by Michelangelo include Raphael, whose monumental treatment of the figure in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/School_of_Athens" title="School of Athens">School of Athens</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Expulsion_of_Heliodorus_from_the_Temple" title="The Expulsion of Heliodorus from the Temple">The Expulsion of Heliodorus from the Temple</a></i> owes much to Michelangelo, and whose fresco of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prophet_Isaiah_(Raphael)" title="The Prophet Isaiah (Raphael)">Isaiah</a></i> in Sant'Agostino closely imitates the older master's prophets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Other artists, such as <a href="/wiki/Pontormo" title="Pontormo">Pontormo</a>, drew on the writhing forms of the <i>Last Judgment</i> and the frescoes of the Capella Paolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sistine Chapel ceiling was a work of unprecedented grandeur, both for its architectonic forms, to be imitated by many <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> ceiling painters, and also for the wealth of its inventiveness in the study of figures. Vasari wrote:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The work has proved a veritable beacon to our art, of inestimable benefit to all painters, restoring light to a world that for centuries had been plunged into darkness. Indeed, painters no longer need to seek for new inventions, novel attitudes, clothed figures, fresh ways of expression, different arrangements, or sublime subjects, for this work contains every perfection possible under those headings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasari_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vasari-110">[110]</a></sup></p></blockquote>

<dl><dd>a. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1041539562">.mw-parser-output .citation{word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}</style><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_anone"><a href="#ref_anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Michelangelo's father marks the date as 6 March 1474 in the Florentine manner <i>ab Incarnatione</i>. However, in the Roman manner, <i>ab Nativitate</i>, it is 1475.</dd>
<dd>b. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_bnone"><a href="#ref_bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Sources disagree as to how old Michelangelo was when he departed for school. De Tolnay writes that it was at ten years old while Sedgwick notes in her translation of Condivi that Michelangelo was seven.</dd>
<dd>c. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_cnone"><a href="#ref_cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> The <a href="/wiki/Strozzi_family" title="Strozzi family">Strozzi family</a> acquired the sculpture <i>Hercules</i>. <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Strozzi_the_Younger" title="Filippo Strozzi the Younger">Filippo Strozzi</a> sold it to <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> in 1529. In 1594, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a> installed it in the Jardin d'Estang at <a href="/wiki/Fontainebleau" title="Fontainebleau">Fontainebleau</a> where it disappeared in 1713 when the Jardin d'Estange was destroyed.</dd>
<dd>d. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1041539562" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_dnone"><a href="#ref_dnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Vasari makes no mention of this episode and <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Giovio" title="Paolo Giovio">Paolo Giovio</a>'s <i>Life of Michelangelo</i> indicates that Michelangelo tried to pass the statue off as an antique himself.</dd></dl>

<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="#CITEREFGayford2013">Gayford (2013)</a>, "Whatever the strength of his feelings, Michelangelo's relationship with Tommaso de'Cavalieri is unlikely to have been a physical, sexual affair. For one thing, it was acted out through poems and images that were far from secret. Even if we do not choose to believe Michelangelo's protestations of the chastity of his behaviour, Tommaso's high social position and the relatively public nature of their relationship make it improbable that it was not platonic."</span>
